===== Response [ID: 3c56c426] [2026-01-25 10:03:14] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze the game step by step to determine the optimal strategy for Player 1.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

We are Player 1, and the other player is Player 2.

The payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff in each cell) is:

|              | P2: A0 | P2: A1 |
|--------------|--------|--------|
| **P1: A0**   | 5      | 0      |
| **P1: A1**   | 3      | 3      |

So:
- If both choose A0: (5, 5)
- If P1: A0, P2: A1: (0, 3)
- If P1: A1, P2: A0: (3, 0)
- If both choose A1: (3, 3)

---

### Step 2: Identify the Type of Game

This is a simultaneous-move game with asymmetric payoffs. We are to find a Nash equilibrium, ideally a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium (MSNE), since pure strategies may not be stable.

Let’s check for pure strategy equilibria:

- (A0, A0): Is this stable?
  - P1 gets 5. If P1 deviates to A1, and P2 still plays A0, P1 gets 3 < 5 → no incentive.
  - P2 gets 5. If P2 deviates to A1, P1 still plays A0 → P2 gets 0 < 5 → no incentive.
  → (A0, A0) is a Nash equilibrium.

- (A1, A1): 
  - P1 gets 3. If P1 deviates to A0 while P2 plays A1 → P1 gets 0 < 3 → no incentive.
  - P2 gets 3. If P2 deviates to A0 while P1 plays A1 → P2 gets 0 < 3 → no incentive.
  → (A1, A1) is also a Nash equilibrium.

- (A0, A1): P1 gets 0. If P1 deviates to A1, gets 3 > 0 → incentive to deviate → not equilibrium.

- (A1, A0): P1 gets 3. If P1 deviates to A0, gets 5 > 3 → incentive → not equilibrium.

So both (A0, A0) and (A1, A1) are pure strategy Nash equilibria.

However, a player might want to avoid risk or maximize expected payoff depending on beliefs about the other player’s behavior.

But since both players are rational and want to maximize their own points, and the game is symmetric in structure (though not in payoffs), we must ask: what is the best strategy, especially if we cannot coordinate?

But note: (A0, A0) gives 5 to each — better than (A1, A1)'s 3.
So (A0, A0) is the socially optimal outcome.

But if each player is only self-interested, and there’s a chance the other will play A1, they might hesitate.

Now consider: is there a mixed strategy equilibrium?

Let’s suppose Player 2 plays A0 with probability p, A1 with probability (1−p).

Player 1’s expected payoff for choosing A0:
→ 5*p + 0*(1−p) = 5p

Player 1’s expected payoff for choosing A1:
→ 3*p + 3*(1−p) = 3p + 3 − 3p = 3

To make Player 1 indifferent between A0 and A1 (so that they are willing to randomize), set:
5p = 3 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 2 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%, then Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Now, from Player 2’s perspective, if Player 1 plays A0 with probability q, A1 with (1−q), what is Player 2’s payoff?

Player 2’s payoffs:
- If Player 2 plays A0: 5*q + 0*(1−q) = 5q
- If Player 2 plays A1: 3*q + 3*(1−q) = 3

Set equal for indifference:
5q = 3 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

So in mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, **both players play A0 with probability 0.6, A1 with 0.4**.

Thus, the equilibrium strategy is:
- Play A0 with 60%
- Play A1 with 40%

Now, is this better than just playing pure A0?

Let’s suppose the other player is rational and plays the MSNE. Then their strategy is 60% A0, 40% A1.

If I play pure A0:
- Expected payoff = 5*(0.6) + 0*(0.4) = 3.0

If I play pure A1:
- Expected payoff = 3*(0.6) + 3*(0.4) = 1.8 + 1.2 = 3.0

Same.

If I play mixed: still 3.0 (since in equilibrium, all strategies give same expected payoff)

So no difference.

But if I play pure A0 and the other player does **not** play MSNE, I might do better.

However, in game theory, we assume both players are rational and will play equilibrium strategies. So if the other player is rational, they will play the MSNE (60% A0, 40% A1), and then playing A0 or A1 gives me 3.0 expected points.

But wait — there is another pure strategy equilibrium: (A0, A0). So if I play A0, and I believe the other player will also play A0, then I get 5.

But the risk is: if I play A0, and the other player plays A1 (which they might, if not coordinated), I get 0.

Similarly, if I play A1, I get at least 3, no matter what.

So A1 is a "safe" strategy — guaranteed 3, whereas A0 is risky: could get 5 or 0.

But if I expect the other player to coordinate on A0, then A0 is better.

However, in the absence of communication, there is no guarantee of coordination.

So from a **maximin** perspective: maximize minimum possible payoff.

- A0: min = 0 (if opponent plays A1)
- A1: min = 3 (if opponent plays A1 or A0)

So A1 dominates A0 in terms of worst-case outcome.

But we may not want to be risk-averse.

Another perspective: is there a **risk-dominant** equilibrium?

In coordination games, sometimes people pick the equilibrium that is safer.

(A0, A0) gives 5 vs 5 → better outcome.
(A1, A1) gives 3 vs 3.

But (A0, A0) is risk-dominant if the expected payoff from playing A0 is higher, given the opponent might miscoordinate.

Let’s compute: if I play A0, and opponent plays A0 with probability p, A1 with 1−p.

My expected payoff: 5p + 0(1−p) = 5p

If I play A1: 3p + 3(1−p) = 3

So I prefer A0 over A1 if 5p > 3 → p > 0.6

So if I believe the opponent plays A0 with probability > 60%, I prefer A0.

Similarly, I prefer A1 if p < 0.6.

If I believe they are equally likely to play A0 or A1 (p = 0.5), then:
- A0: 5*0.5 = 2.5
- A1: 3
→ A1 better.

So only if I believe opponent plays A0 > 60% of the time, I prefer A0.

But since (A0, A0) is an equilibrium, if both players expect the other to play A0, they both want to play A0.

But if one player is unsure, there’s a risk.

Now, consider the **best response**.

In a mixed strategy equilibrium, we found p = 0.6 for the opponent to make us indifferent.

But as Player 1, what should *I* do?

The key question: **Is the game a coordination game?**

Yes: both players benefit from matching.

But the payoffs are different.

Let’s think differently: what if I want to maximize my expected payoff **assuming the other player is also playing optimally**?

Then they will play the MSNE: A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%.

Then my best response is any mixture — all give same payoff: 3.

So I’m indifferent.

But suppose I want to **maximize worst-case** payoff: then pick A1 → I get at least 3.

If I pick A0, I could get 0.

Thus, **A1 is safer**.

But (A0, A0) is better if we both play A0.

But since we cannot communicate, and the game is symmetric, the only stable equilibrium that doesn’t rely on coordination is the mixed one.

But wait: (A0, A0) is also a Nash equilibrium — but it's Pareto superior.

However, in the absence of communication, both players might not coordinate on it, and (A1, A1) remains an equilibrium.

So is (A0, A0) stable? Only if both players believe the other will play A0.

But if I think the other player might be irrational or might play A1, I might not risk A0.

But here’s a thought: **Player 1’s best choice depends on beliefs.**

But since we are to choose a strategy to maximize expected payoff under rational play by opponent, and we are to **output a single fixed probability distribution (over A0 and A1)**, we need to pick one.

Now: suppose the other player is also a rational player. Then they will also play optimally.

In a symmetric game, the symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium is the only symmetric equilibrium that doesn't depend on coordination.

And in this case, as we found: both players should play A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%.

This makes both players indifferent, and neither has incentive to deviate.

Moreover, if I deviate, say, play A0 with 100%, and the opponent plays 60% A0, then:
- My expected payoff: 5*0.6 + 0*0.4 = 3.0 → same as before.
- No gain.

Similarly, if I play A1 only, I get 3.0.

So no profitable deviation.

Thus, **the symmetric mixed strategy equilibrium is a valid and stable choice**.

Therefore, the optimal strategy for Player 1, assuming rational opponent, is to play:
- A0: 60%
- A1: 40%

This is the symmetric MSNE.

Alternatively, could I do better by playing pure A0?

Only if the other player plays A0 more than 60% of the time — but if they are playing optimally, they play 60% A0.

So no.

Therefore, to maximize expected payoff under rational play, **play 60% A0, 40% A1**.

But is this the only equilibrium? No — but it is the one that is symmetric and doesn’t require coordination.

In decision-making games without communication, mixed strategies are often considered the "safe" bet.

Moreover, if I play pure A0, and the other player plays pure A1, I get 0 — which is disastrous.

While if I play 60% A0, 40% A1, I am guaranteed an expected payoff of 3.0 regardless of the other player’s strategy (in equilibrium), and I can’t be exploited easily.

Therefore, the recommended strategy is the symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium.

---

### Final Decision:

Play A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%.

This satisfies:
- Sum = 100
- Uses integer percentage points
- Matches optimal play under rationality

---

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break down the problem step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Game Structure

This is a simultaneous two-player game with two actions: A0 and A1.

You are Player 1. The other player is Player 2.

Each player independently chooses a probability distribution over A0 and A1:
- You choose: P(A0) = p, P(A1) = 1−p
- Opponent chooses: P(A0) = q, P(A1) = 1−q

After choosing, the outcome is determined randomly according to the joint probabilities.

---

### Step 2: Payoff Matrix

Let’s write the payoffs from Player 1’s perspective:

|         | Opponent: A0 (q) | Opponent: A1 (1−q) |
|---------|------------------|--------------------|
| **You: A0 (p)** | (5, 5)           | (0, 3)             |
| **You: A1 (1−p)** | (3, 0)           | (3, 3)             |

We can compute your expected payoff as a function of p and q.

Your expected payoff:
- With probability p·q: you both pick A0 → you get 5
- With probability p·(1−q): you pick A0, opponent picks A1 → you get 0
- With probability (1−p)·q: you pick A1, opponent picks A0 → you get 3
- With probability (1−p)·(1−q): you both pick A1 → you get 3

So your total expected payoff is:
> E[Payoff] = p·q·5 + p·(1−q)·0 + (1−p)·q·3 + (1−p)·(1−q)·3

Simplify:
> E = 5pq + 0 + 3(1−p)q + 3(1−p)(1−q)

Now expand each term:
- 5pq
- 3q(1−p) = 3q − 3pq
- 3(1−p)(1−q) = 3(1 − p − q + pq) = 3 − 3p − 3q + 3pq

Add them together:
> E = 5pq + (3q − 3pq) + (3 − 3p − 3q + 3pq)  
> E = 5pq − 3pq + 3pq + 3q − 3q + 3 − 3p  
> E = (5pq − 3pq + 3pq) + 0q + 3 − 3p  
> E = 5pq + 3 − 3p

Wait — actually, let's recheck:

Break down again:

1. 5pq  
2. 3(1−p)q = 3q − 3pq  
3. 3(1−p)(1−q) = 3(1 − q − p + pq) = 3 − 3q − 3p + 3pq  

Sum all:
- Constant terms: 3
- p terms: −3p
- q terms: 3q − 3q = 0
- pq terms: 5pq − 3pq + 3pq = 5pq

So total:
> E = 3 − 3p + 5pq

Yes! Therefore:

> **Expected payoff for Player 1 = 3 − 3p + 5pq**

We want to maximize this expectation. But note: p is your choice (what you control), and q is the opponent’s choice (unknown to you). Since the game is simultaneous, you must assume the opponent is trying to minimize your payoff or play optimally too.

But since you're supposed to **maximize your own points**, and the opponent also tries to maximize their score (but we don't know how they will behave), we should consider what happens in equilibrium.

Let’s now check if there is a **Nash Equilibrium**.

---

### Step 3: Find Best Response and Nash Equilibrium

Assume Player 2 uses a mixed strategy q (probability of A0), and we want to find Player 1’s best response.

From above:  
E1 = 3 − 3p + 5pq

We can treat this as a function of p, given q.

Let’s take derivative w.r.t p (for continuous case):

dE1/dp = −3 + 5q

Set to zero for maximum:
> −3 + 5q = 0 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

Interpretation:
- If q < 0.6, then dE1/dp < 0 → increasing p decreases payoff → best to set p = 0
- If q > 0.6, then dE1/dp > 0 → increasing p increases payoff → best to set p = 1
- If q = 0.6, then derivative is 0 → any p gives same payoff? Wait, not exactly — but indifferent.

This suggests that Player 1’s best response depends on q:

- If q < 0.6 → best to play A1 only → p = 0
- If q > 0.6 → best to play A0 only → p = 1
- If q = 0.6 → indifferent between pure strategies

Now, what about Player 2?

Let’s compute Player 2’s expected payoff (from their perspective).

Player 2’s payoff:
|         | You: A0 (p) | You: A1 (1−p) |
|---------|-------------|---------------|
| **Opp: A0 (q)** | 5           | 0             |
| **Opp: A1 (1−q)** | 3           | 3             |

So Player 2’s expected payoff:
> E2 = q·p·5 + q·(1−p)·0 + (1−q)·p·3 + (1−q)·(1−p)·3

Simplify:
= 5pq + 0 + 3p(1−q) + 3(1−q)(1−p)

Expand:
= 5pq + 3p − 3pq + 3(1−q)(1−p)

Now expand last term: 3(1 − q − p + pq) = 3 − 3q − 3p + 3pq

So total:
= 5pq − 3pq + 3pq + 3p − 3p + 3 − 3q  
Wait, let's do it carefully:

Terms:
- 5pq
- 3p − 3pq
- 3 − 3q − 3p + 3pq

Add:
- pq: 5pq − 3pq + 3pq = 5pq
- p: 3p − 3p = 0
- q: −3q
- constant: 3

So:
> E2 = 5pq − 3q + 3

Now, Player 2 wants to maximize this. Treat p as fixed (your choice), and q is theirs.

Take derivative w.r.t q:
> dE2/dq = 5p − 3

Set to zero: 5p − 3 = 0 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So:
- If p < 0.6 → dE2/dq < 0 → decreasing q increases payoff → best q = 0
- If p > 0.6 → dE2/dq > 0 → increasing q helps → best q = 1
- If p = 0.6 → indifferent

So Player 2’s best response:
- p < 0.6 → q = 0
- p > 0.6 → q = 1
- p = 0.6 → any q

Now, let’s look for **Nash Equilibrium**: a pair (p, q) such that both players are playing best responses to each other.

Let’s suppose p = 0.6. Then Player 2 is indifferent, so could play any q.

But if q = 0.6 (as per earlier), then Player 1 is indifferent.

So the **mixed-strategy Nash equilibrium** occurs at:
- p = 0.6 (you choose A0 with 60% chance)
- q = 0.6 (opponent chooses A0 with 60% chance)

Why? Because:
- At p = 0.6, Player 2 is indifferent → any q is best response — including q = 0.6
- At q = 0.6, Player 1 is indifferent → any p is best response — including p = 0.6

Therefore, (p=0.6, q=0.6) is a Nash equilibrium.

Is this the only one? There might be others, but this is symmetric and stable.

If you play p = 0.6, and opponent plays q = 0.6, then neither has incentive to deviate.

Moreover, since you’re maximizing your own score, playing a strategy that puts you in a Nash equilibrium is optimal **if** the opponent is rational and aims to maximize their own payoff.

Also, note: in mixed-strategy Nash equilibrium, you are randomizing to make the opponent indifferent — and vice versa.

Thus, **playing p = 0.6 (i.e., 60% on A0, 40% on A1)** is your optimal strategy.

---

### Step 4: Convert to Required Output Format

You must output:
- A JSON object
- Keys: "A0", "A1"
- Values: integers representing percentages (summing to 100)

So:
- A0: 60
- A1: 40

Sum: 100 → valid

===== Response [ID: df628385] [2026-01-25 10:03:21] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze the game step by step to determine the optimal strategy.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

We are Player 1. The other player is Player 2. The payoffs are as follows:

| Your Action \ Other's Action | A0     | A1     |
|-------------------------------|--------|--------|
| **A0**                        | 5, 5   | 0, 3   |
| **A1**                        | 3, 0   | 3, 3   |

So the payoff for Player 1 is:

- (A0, A0): 5
- (A0, A1): 0
- (A1, A0): 3
- (A1, A1): 3

---

### Step 2: Look for dominant strategies

Check if either player has a dominant strategy.

**For Player 1:**

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - A0 gives 5
  - A1 gives 3 → A0 is better
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - A0 gives 0
  - A1 gives 3 → A1 is better

So Player 1 has **no dominant strategy** — the best choice depends on what Player 2 does.

**For Player 2:**

- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - A0 gives 5
  - A1 gives 3 → A0 is better
- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - A0 gives 0
  - A1 gives 3 → A1 is better

So Player 2 also has **no dominant strategy**.

---

### Step 3: Look for Nash Equilibrium (NE)

We are looking for a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, where each player chooses a probability such that the other player cannot gain by unilaterally changing their strategy.

Let:
- Player 1 chooses A0 with probability **p**
- Player 2 chooses A0 with probability **q**

We will find **p** such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1, and **q** such that Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

#### First: Find **q** that makes Player 1 indifferent between A0 and A1

Player 1's expected payoff:

- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - Expected payoff = q × 5 + (1−q) × 0 = 5q

- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - Expected payoff = q × 3 + (1−q) × 3 = 3

Set equal for indifference:
5q = 3 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 2 plays A0 with probability 60%, then Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

#### Now: Find **p** that makes Player 2 indifferent between A0 and A1

Player 2's expected payoff:

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - Payoff = p × 5 + (1−p) × 0 = 5p

- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - Payoff = p × 3 + (1−p) × 3 = 3

Set equal:
5p = 3 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 60%, then Player 2 is indifferent.

---

### Step 4: Conclusion – Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium

The symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium is:
- Player 1 chooses A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%
- Player 2 chooses A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%

In this equilibrium, neither player can improve their expected payoff by changing their strategy unilaterally.

Since the game is symmetric in this way and we are playing to **maximize our own points**, and assuming we cannot coordinate with the other player, the best strategy is to play the equilibrium strategy.

Hence, **we should choose A0 with 60% probability and A1 with 40%**.

This is the optimal response in the absence of communication or information about the other player's choice.

---

### Final Answer:

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

===== Response [ID: a9add9b6] [2026-01-25 10:03:25] =====
agent: google/gemini-3-flash-preview(CoT)#P2
response:
To maximize the total points in this decision-making game, we must analyze the payoff structure for Player 2 (myself) and Player 1.

The payoffs for Player 2 are:
- (A0, A0): 5 points (if both choose A0)
- (A0, A1): 0 points (if Player 2 chooses A0 and Player 1 chooses A1)
- (A1, A0): 3 points (if Player 2 chooses A1 and Player 1 chooses A0)
- (A1, A1): 3 points (if both choose A1)

Similarly, for Player 1:
- (A0, A0): 5 points
- (A0, A1): 3 points (if Player 1 chooses A1)
- (A1, A0): 0 points (if Player 1 chooses A0 and Player 2 chooses A1)
- (A1, A1): 3 points

This is a classic coordination game, specifically a version of the **Stag Hunt**. There are two pure-strategy Nash equilibria:
1.  **(A0, A0)**: Provides the highest payoff (5 points each). This is the **payoff-dominant** equilibrium.
2.  **(A1, A1)**: Provides a lower but guaranteed payoff for the player who chooses it (3 points). This is the **risk-dominant** equilibrium.

A1 is the "safe" action: by choosing A1, a player is guaranteed 3 points regardless of the other's choice. A0 is "risky": it yields 5 points if both coordinate, but 0 points if the other player opts for the safe strategy.

To decide between A0 and A1, we compare the expected payoffs. Let $p$ be the probability that the other player chooses A0. The expected payoff for A0 is $5p + 0(1-p) = 5p$, and the expected payoff for A1 is $3p + 3(1-p) = 3$. Choosing A0 is mathematically superior if $5p > 3$, or $p > 0.6$.

In many strategic environments, especially where players are prompted to maximize total points, there is a strong incentive to coordinate on the payoff-dominant equilibrium (A0, A0). If both players recognize the mutual benefit of receiving 5 points instead of 3, they will converge on A0.

Therefore, choosing A0 with 100% probability is the most effective way to reach the highest possible score, assuming the other player is also a rational actor seeking to maximize their total points.

===== Response [ID: 85f37a91] [2026-01-25 10:03:30] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze this step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

We are Player 1. The payoff matrix (from Player 1's perspective) is:

|               | Player 2: A0 | Player 2: A1 |
|---------------|--------------|--------------|
| **Player 1: A0** | 5            | 0            |
| **Player 1: A1** | 3            | 3            |

From Player 2's perspective (for completeness):

|               | Player 2: A0 | Player 2: A1 |
|---------------|--------------|--------------|
| **Player 1: A0** | 5            | 3            |
| **Player 1: A1** | 0            | 3            |

We want to **maximize our own expected points**, so we’re solving for the optimal mixed strategy (probability distribution over A0 and A1) that maximizes Player 1's expected payoff, **assuming the other player is also rational and will play to maximize their own payoff**.

---

### Step 2: Check for Pure Strategy Nash Equilibria

Let’s see if there are any pure strategy Nash equilibria.

- (A0, A0): Player 1 gets 5, Player 2 gets 5.  
  - If Player 1 deviates to A1, gets 3 < 5 → no incentive.
  - If Player 2 deviates to A1, gets 3 < 5 → no incentive.  
  → **This is a Nash equilibrium**.

- (A1, A1): Player 1 gets 3, Player 2 gets 3.  
  - If Player 1 deviates to A0, gets 0 < 3 → no incentive.
  - If Player 2 deviates to A0, gets 0 < 3 → no incentive.  
  → **Also a Nash equilibrium**.

- (A0, A1): Player 1 gets 0, Player 2 gets 3.  
  - Player 1 would prefer to switch to A1 → gets 3 > 0 → not equilibrium.

- (A1, A0): Player 1 gets 3, Player 2 gets 0.  
  - Player 2 would prefer to switch to A1 → gets 3 > 0 → not equilibrium.

So there are **two pure strategy Nash equilibria**: (A0, A0) and (A1, A1).

But note: in (A0, A0), both players get 5 — the highest possible payoff for both. In (A1, A1), both get only 3. So (A0, A0) is better for both.

But here’s the catch: **We can't coordinate** with the other player. We need to choose a strategy that maximizes **our own expected payoff**, assuming the other player is also rational and will best respond.

So if we choose A0, the other player might respond with A0 (to avoid getting only 3 when they could get 5), but if they think we’ll play A0, they might prefer A0.

But we need to consider **what the other player will do given our strategy**.

---

### Step 3: Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium Analysis

Let’s suppose both players use mixed strategies.

Let:
- $ p $ = probability that Player 1 plays A0
- $ q $ = probability that Player 2 plays A0

We want to find a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium.

We look for a strategy for Player 1 such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 (if q is chosen optimally), and vice versa.

But since the game is symmetric in structure (though not symmetric in payoffs), let’s do the calculation.

#### From Player 1’s perspective: What is the best response?

Player 1 wants to maximize expected payoff.

Let’s define expected payoff for Player 1 given mixed strategies.

If Player 1 plays A0 with probability $ p $, and Player 2 plays A0 with probability $ q $, then:

Player 1’s expected payoff:
$$
E_1 = p \cdot [q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3]
$$
$$
= p(5q) + (1 - p)(3)
$$
$$
= 5pq + 3 - 3p
$$

Now, suppose Player 2 is trying to maximize their own expected payoff.

Player 2's payoff:
- If they play A0: $ p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 0 = 5p $
- If they play A1: $ p \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3 $

So Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 when:
$$
5p = 3 \Rightarrow p = 3/5 = 0.6
$$

So if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 0.6, Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Similarly, if Player 2 plays A0 with probability $ q $, then Player 1’s expected payoff for playing A0 is:
$$
q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0 = 5q
$$
For playing A1:
$$
q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3 = 3
$$

Player 1 is indifferent when:
$$
5q = 3 \Rightarrow q = 3/5 = 0.6
$$

So at Nash equilibrium, both players play A0 with probability 0.6, A1 with 0.4.

Let’s verify:

- If both play A0 with 60% chance, then:
  - Player 1's expected payoff:
    - A0: gets 5 with prob 0.6, 0 with prob 0.4 → 5×0.6 + 0×0.4 = 3
    - A1: gets 3 regardless → 3
    → so Player 1 is indifferent → mixed strategy is valid.
  - Similarly for Player 2.

So **(p = 0.6, q = 0.6)** is a **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

But we are asked to **maximize our own total points**.

In this equilibrium, Player 1's expected payoff is:
- From above: $ E_1 = 5pq + 3 - 3p $
- Plug in $ p = 0.6, q = 0.6 $:
  $$
  E_1 = 5×0.6×0.6 + 3 - 3×0.6 = 1.8 + 3 - 1.8 = 3
  $$

But we can do better?

Wait — what if we **deviate** from the equilibrium?

Suppose we play A0 with probability 1 (i.e., always A0).

Then what will Player 2 do?  
Player 2’s best response:

- If we always play A0:
  - If Player 2 plays A0 → they get 5
  - If Player 2 plays A1 → they get 3
  → So Player 2 will play A0 (to maximize their payoff).

Then outcome: (A0, A0) → we get 5 points.

Similarly, if we play A1 with probability 1:
- Player 2’s best response: if we play A1 always, then:
  - If they play A0 → get 0
  - If they play A1 → get 3
  → so they play A1 → we get 3.

So best response to (A0, A0) is (A0, A0) — so if we play A0 deterministically, and Player 2 is rational, they will play A0 too — giving us 5.

But Player 2 could be myopic — but we’re told to assume both players are rational.

So if **I** play A0 for sure, then Player 2's best response is to play A0, and I get 5.

In contrast, in the mixed equilibrium, I only get 3.

So **why would I play a mixed strategy if playing pure A0 yields 5**?

But — is that stable?

Wait — let’s think carefully.

Suppose I deviate and play A0 always.

Then Player 2, knowing I play A0 always, can choose:
- A0: gets 5
- A1: gets 3

So they will choose A0.

So outcome: (A0, A0) → I get 5.

So playing A0 always gives me 5.

But what if Player 2 is not sure I will play A0? But the problem doesn’t say anything about beliefs — it says we choose probabilities simultaneously, and everything is in expectation.

But we are to **maximize our points**, so we must choose a strategy that yields the highest expected payoff **assuming the other player best responds**.

So if I choose $ p = 100 $ (A0 with 100% probability), then Player 2's best response is $ q = 100 $ (A0), and I get 5.

Similarly, if I choose $ p = 0 $, Player 2 plays A1 → I get 3.

If I choose $ p = 50 $, Player 2’s best response?

Player 2’s expected payoff:
- A0: $ 0.5×5 + 0.5×0 = 2.5 $
- A1: $ 0.5×3 + 0.5×3 = 3 $
→ So Player 2 prefers A1 → q = 0

Then my expected payoff: I play A0 with 0.5, A1 with 0.5; Player 2 plays A1.
- A0 vs A1: I get 0
- A1 vs A1: I get 3
→ Expected: 0.5×0 + 0.5×3 = 1.5

Worse than 5.

Only when I play A0 with probability 1 do I get 5, because then Player 2 best responds with A0.

But is there a **risk**?

Suppose Player 2 is not rational and might play A1 even if I play A0.

But we are told to assume rational players — so we can assume best response.

Therefore, the **best strategy for me is to play A0 with probability 100%**, because:
- It leads to a pure Nash equilibrium (A0, A0), giving me 5 points.
- Player 2, being rational, will respond by playing A0.
- Any deviation (e.g., mixing) risks getting lower expected payoff if Player 2 responds with A1.

But — what if Player 2 reasons the same way? They might also want to play A0 to get 5.

So the strategy (A0, A0) is a stable outcome.

Is there any incentive for me to mix?

Suppose I play A0 with probability 100%. I get 5.

Suppose I play A0 with 99%, A1 with 1%. Then:
- Player 2’s best response?
  - If Player 2 plays A0: expected payoff = $ 0.99×5 + 0.01×0 = 4.95 $
  - If Player 2 plays A1: $ 0.99×3 + 0.01×3 = 3 $
  → So Player 2 still plays A0
  → I get: with prob 0.99, A0 and A0 → 5; with prob 0.01, A1 and A0 → 3
  → expected: 0.99×5 + 0.01×3 = 4.95 + 0.03 = 4.98 < 5

So worse than playing A0 always.

Any mixing with A1 reduces expected payoff (because it gives Player 2 incentive to possibly deviate, but in this case they still prefer A0, but I get a small chance of getting only 3).

So to **maximize minimum payoff** or **expected payoff**, playing A0 with 100% is better.

Therefore, the rational choice is to play **A0 with 100% probability**.

Wait — but let’s consider a deeper point.

In game theory, if both players are rational and know each other is rational, they can **coordinate on the efficient Nash equilibrium** (A0, A0).

Since (A0, A0) yields 5 to me, and I can achieve it by simply playing A0, and the other player will respond rationally by playing A0, then **this is the best possible outcome**.

So I should commit to A0.

Conclusion: **Play A0 with probability 100%**.

This gives me 5 points (with certainty), assuming the other player plays optimally.

Any other strategy risks giving me less.

---

### Final Decision

We choose:
- A0: 100
- A1: 0

This is valid (sums to 100), integers, correct format.

===== Response [ID: c8b9a076] [2026-01-25 10:03:57] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break this down step by step to determine the optimal probability distribution for Player 1 (you) to maximize your expected points.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

We are in a two-player simultaneous game. Let’s construct the payoff matrix from **your perspective (Player 1)**.

| Your Action \ Other's Action | A0         | A1         |
|------------------------------|------------|------------|
| **A0**                        | 5          | 0          |
| **A1**                        | 3          | 3          |

So if you play A0:
- If opponent plays A0 → you get 5
- If opponent plays A1 → you get 0

If you play A1:
- If opponent plays A0 → you get 3
- If opponent plays A1 → you get 3

Now, we must consider that **both players choose probabilities simultaneously**, and the outcome is drawn randomly based on those probabilities.

Let:
- $ p $ = probability you (Player 1) choose A0 → then A1 is $ 1 - p $
- $ q $ = probability the other player chooses A0 → then A1 is $ 1 - q $

---

### Step 2: Expected payoff for you (Player 1)

Your expected payoff is computed as:

$$
E = P(\text{you A0 and opponent A0}) \times 5 + 
P(\text{you A0 and opponent A1}) \times 0 +
P(\text{you A1 and opponent A0}) \times 3 +
P(\text{you A1 and opponent A1}) \times 3
$$

$$
E = p \cdot q \cdot 5 + p \cdot (1 - q) \cdot 0 + (1 - p) \cdot q \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot (1 - q) \cdot 3
$$

Simplify:

$$
E = 5pq + 0 + 3(1 - p)q + 3(1 - p)(1 - q)
$$

Break it down:
- $ 5pq $
- $ 3(1 - p)q = 3q - 3pq $
- $ 3(1 - p)(1 - q) = 3(1 - q - p + pq) = 3 - 3q - 3p + 3pq $

Now sum all terms:

$$
E = 5pq + (3q - 3pq) + (3 - 3q - 3p + 3pq)
$$

Combine like terms:
- $ pq $: $ 5pq - 3pq + 3pq = 5pq $
- $ q $: $ 3q - 3q = 0 $
- Constants: $ 3 $
- $ p $: $ -3p $

So:

$$
E = 5pq - 3p + 3
$$

That’s your expected payoff.

$$
E(p, q) = 5pq - 3p + 3
$$

You want to **maximize your expected payoff**, but **you don't know $ q $**, the other player’s strategy.

But since this is a decision-making game and both players choose simultaneously and rationally, we should look for a **Nash equilibrium** — a pair of strategies where neither player can gain by unilaterally changing their strategy.

---

### Step 3: Find the Nash Equilibrium

We assume the other player is also trying to maximize their own payoff. Let’s find symmetric or mixed-strategy equilibrium.

Let’s suppose both players use mixed strategies:
- You: play A0 with probability $ p $
- Opponent: plays A0 with probability $ q $

We need to find values of $ p $ and $ q $ such that neither has an incentive to deviate.

#### First: What is the opponent’s best response?

From the opponent’s perspective (Player 2), their payoffs are:

| Their Action \ Your Action | A0        | A1        |
|----------------------------|-----------|-----------|
| **A0**                     | 5         | 0         |
| **A1**                     | 3         | 3         |

So their expected payoff if they play A0:
- $ p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 0 = 5p $

If they play A1:
- $ p \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3 $

They will choose A0 only if $ 5p > 3 $ → $ p > 0.6 $
- If $ p < 0.6 $, they prefer A1 (get 3 vs less than 3)
- If $ p = 0.6 $, they are indifferent.

To make them indifferent between A0 and A1, set $ 5p = 3 $ → $ p = 0.6 $

In a Nash equilibrium, each player must be indifferent given the other’s strategy, or not have an incentive to switch.

So if you play A0 with $ p = 0.6 $, then the opponent is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Thus, they can play any mix — but to make it stable, let’s see what happens if **both play mixed strategies** where each is indifferent.

So suppose the opponent uses $ q $. Then your expected payoff was:

$$
E = 5pq - 3p + 3
$$

But we can now **choose $ p $** to maximize $ E $, knowing that in equilibrium, the opponent’s $ q $ will be chosen optimally.

Alternatively, let's **find a symmetric mixed strategy equilibrium**.

Suppose both players use the same mixed strategy: play A0 with probability $ x $, A1 with $ 1 - x $.

In equilibrium, your strategy should make the opponent indifferent between A0 and A1.

From above, opponent is indifferent when your $ p = 0.6 $

So in equilibrium, **you should play A0 with probability 0.6**, so the opponent is indifferent, and thus they would use any strategy — but to make our strategy equilibrium, we require that **they are willing to match**.

Wait — better approach: find $ p $ such that **if the opponent plays optimal response**, you are best off.

But here's another way: Suppose you fix your $ p $, and the opponent chooses $ q $ to maximize their own payoff.

We already found: opponent chooses A0 iff $ p > 0.6 $, A1 iff $ p < 0.6 $, indifferent at $ p = 0.6 $

So if you play $ p < 0.6 $, opponent prefers A1 → $ q = 0 $
Then your expected payoff becomes:

$$
E = 5p(0) - 3p + 3 = 0 - 3p + 3 = 3 - 3p
$$

Since $ p < 0.6 $, this is $ > 3 - 3(0.6) = 3 - 1.8 = 1.2 $

Wait — actually: if opponent plays $ q = 0 $ (always A1), then your payoff:

- Play A0: get 0 when opponent A1 → 0
- Play A1: get 3 always

So your best move is A1 → get 3.

So expected payoff = 3 (if you play A1 with certainty)

But you’re playing A0 with prob $ p < 0.6 $, so you're mixing.

But the opponent’s behavior depends on your $ p $. So we need to analyze based on $ p $.

Let me reframe.

Let’s suppose you pick a value of $ p $ (your probability of A0). The opponent will best respond.

Case 1: $ p > 0.6 $  
→ Opponent gets more from A0 (5p > 3), so they pick $ q = 1 $ (A0 always)  
Then your expected payoff:
- With prob $ p $, you play A0 → get 5 (since opponent A0)
- With prob $ 1-p $, you play A1 → get 3
→ $ E = p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 5p + 3 - 3p = 2p + 3 $

Since $ p > 0.6 $, maximum is when $ p = 1 $ → $ E = 2(1) + 3 = 5 $

But can you do better?

Case 2: $ p < 0.6 $  
→ Opponent gets more from A1 (3 > 5p), so they pick $ q = 0 $ (A1 always)  
Then your payoffs:
- If you play A0: get 0 (opponent A1)
- If you play A1: get 3

So best action is A1 → so you should play A1 with probability 1 → $ p = 0 $

But you are playing $ p < 0.6 $

Expected payoff:
- With prob $ p $: A0 → 0
- With prob $ 1-p $: A1 → 3
→ $ E = p \cdot 0 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3 - 3p $

Now, to maximize this, minimize $ p $. Minimum is $ p = 0 $ → $ E = 3 $

Case 3: $ p = 0.6 $  
→ Opponent is indifferent between A0 and A1 → so they can play any $ q $

But since you’re choosing $ p = 0.6 $, let’s compute your expected payoff *regardless* of $ q $, because opponent doesn’t care.

From earlier:  
$ E = 5pq - 3p + 3 $

Plug $ p = 0.6 $:

$$
E = 5(0.6)q - 3(0.6) + 3 = 3q - 1.8 + 3 = 3q + 1.2
$$

Now $ q \in [0,1] $, so:
- Min: $ q = 0 $ → $ E = 1.2 $
- Max: $ q = 1 $ → $ E = 4.2 $

But opponent wants to maximize their *own* payoff.

Opponent’s payoff:
If opponent plays A0: $ 5p = 5(0.6) = 3 $
If opponent plays A1: $ 3 $
So at $ p = 0.6 $, they are indifferent — so they can choose any $ q $.

But since you want to **maximize your expected payoff**, and you know they’ll pick $ q $ to make it **as bad as possible** for you? No — they are rational too, and want to maximize their own payoff, not hurt you.

But since they are indifferent between A0 and A1, they could pick any strategy. But in Nash equilibrium, we assume they can pick any — including one that minimizes *your* payoff? No — they are self-interested.

Wait: They are indifferent — so they may pick any. But since they don’t care, we need to assume they will pick something acceptable.

But **you** want to maximize your **worst-case** outcome? No — you don’t know what they will do.

However, in game theory, in equilibrium, **each player’s strategy makes the other indifferent**.

So ideal equilibrium: you choose $ p $ such that opponent is indifferent → $ p = 0.6 $

And opponent chooses $ q $ such that you are indifferent.

Let’s solve that.

Suppose opponent plays A0 with probability $ q $, A1 with $ 1-q $

Your expected payoff:
- From A0: $ q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0 = 5q $
- From A1: $ q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3 = 3 $

You are indifferent between A0 and A1 when:
$$
5q = 3 \Rightarrow q = 0.6
$$

So if opponent plays A0 with probability $ 0.6 $, you are indifferent → so you can play any mix, including $ p = 0.6 $

Similarly, we already saw that if you play $ p = 0.6 $, opponent is indifferent → so they can play $ q = 0.6 $

So **a Nash equilibrium exists when both players play A0 with probability 0.6**

At this point:
- You are indifferent between A0 and A1 → so randomizing at 0.6 is fine
- Opponent is indifferent between A0 and A1 → so randomizing at 0.6 is fine

So **(p = 0.6, q = 0.6)** is a symmetric mixed-strategy Nash equilibrium.

Now, what is your expected payoff in this equilibrium?

Using earlier formula:

$$
E = 5pq - 3p + 3 = 5(0.6)(0.6) - 3(0.6) + 3 = 5(0.36) - 1.8 + 3 = 1.8 - 1.8 + 3 = 3
$$

So 3 points.

Suppose you play pure A1 (p=0):

- Then opponent sees: your p=0 → so their best response: A1 (since 3 > 5*0 = 0) → q=0
- Then:

Same as equilibrium.

- Opponent sees p=1 → 5p=5 > 3 → so they play A0 → q=1
- You both A0 → payoff: 5 points

Better! 5 points.

No. Why? Because if you play A0 always, opponent plays A0 always → you get 5.

If opponent deviates to A1: you A0, they A1 → they get 3 < 5 → so no incentive

If you deviate to A1: opponent A0 → you get 3 < 5 → no incentive

So yes — **(A0, A0)** is a pure Nash equilibrium.

- Both play A1 → you get 3
- Opponent gets 3

If you deviate to A0: opponent A1 → you get 0 < 3 → no incentive

If opponent deviates to A0: you A1 → enemy A0 → they get 0 < 3 → no incentive

In the absence of communication, players might coordinate on the payoff-dominant equilibrium.

Let's analyze this game step by step.

We are playing a two-player simultaneous-move game, and we are Player 2 (the "you" in the description). We must choose a probability distribution over actions A0 and A1, simultaneously with the other player (Player 1), and our goal is to **maximize our own expected points**, knowing that the other player is also trying to maximize their own payoff.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

Let's write out the payoffs in matrix form, with **Player 1 (you)** as the row player, and **Player 2 (other)** as the column player. Wait — clarification: You are Player 2. So we must be careful with roles.

From the problem:

> "you are playing in the position of Player 2"

So let's define:

- Rows: Player 1 (the other player) chooses A0 or A1.
- Columns: Player 2 (you) chooses A0 or A1.

```

       A0       A1

```

|         | A0 (P1) | A1 (P1) |
|---------|--------|--------|
| A0 (P2) | 5,5    | 0,3    |
| A1 (P2) | 3,0    | 3,3    |

Your payoff is the first number in each pair.

---

### Step 2: Look for Nash Equilibrium

We are supposed to choose a strategy (probability distribution) to maximize our **expected payoff**, assuming the other player is rational and will try to maximize their own payoff. So we should aim for a Nash equilibrium (NE), where neither player has an incentive to deviate.

Let’s suppose:

- Player 1 chooses A0 with probability p, A1 with probability (1−p).
- Player 2 (you) chooses A0 with probability q, A1 with probability (1−q). We aim to find the optimal q.

We want to compute your expected payoff as a function of q, given that Player 1 plays p.

But since we don’t know p, we must find a strategy q that is best response to *any* rational play by Player 1 — or, ideally, find a Nash equilibrium where both players are using optimal mixed strategies.

Let’s use the method of **indifference principle** to find a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium.

---

### Step 3: Find Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium

Assume that Player 1 (the other player) mixes between A0 and A1. For Player 2 (you) to be indifferent between A0 and A1 (and thus able to mix), Player 1 must choose p such that your expected payoff from choosing A0 equals that from choosing A1.

Let’s compute your expected payoff from:

- Choosing A0:  
  - If P1 chooses A0 (prob p): you get 5  
  - If P1 chooses A1 (prob 1−p): you get 0  
  → Expected payoff = 5p + 0(1−p) = 5p

- Choosing A1:  
  - If P1 chooses A0 (prob p): you get 3  
  - If P1 chooses A1 (prob 1−p): you get 3  
  → Expected payoff = 3p + 3(1−p) = 3

Now, for you (Player 2) to be indifferent between A0 and A1, you need:

5p = 3 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 0.6, A1 with 0.4, then your expected payoff is the same for A0 and A1 — you’re indifferent.

Therefore, **a mixed strategy NE occurs when Player 1 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%**.

- Choosing A0:

- If q ≥ 0.6 → P1 plays A0 → you get 5 if you choose A0, 3 if A1 → so you will choose A0 (since 5 > 3). So you will end up playing A0 with probability 1.
- Therefore, in this case, you play A0, P1 plays A0 → you get 5.

Wait — but you're choosing a mixed strategy. But if you choose q ≥ 0.6, P1 responds with A0. Then you have no incentive to play A1 — so you’d choose A0. So effectively, you end up playing A0 with probability 1.

So if you set q ≥ 0.6, then the outcome becomes (A0, A0) → you get 5.

If you set q < 0.6 → P1 plays A1 (since 5q < 3 → A1 gives P1 3 > 5q). So P1 plays A1.

Then, if P1 plays A1, your best response is to play A1 (get 3), since A0 gives you 0.

So you end up playing A1 → outcome is (A1, A1) → you get 3.

So:

- If q ≥ 0.6 → outcome is (A0, A0) → you get 5
- If q < 0.6 → outcome is (A1, A1) → you get 3

Therefore, to maximize your expected payoff, you should choose **q ≥ 0.6**.

And since you want to maximize your payoff, and both choices (q = 0.6, q = 1.0, etc.) lead to you getting 5 (as long as q ≥ 0.6), you are indifferent among them.

But you are constrained to pick **a single probability distribution**.

So the minimal q to get 5 is **q = 60%**.

But you can also choose **q = 100%** (always play A0). Then P1 sees that you play A0 with certainty → then 5q = 5 > 3 → so P1 will play A0 → outcome is (A0, A0) → you get 5.

Similarly, if you play q = 60%, then 5q = 3 → P1 is indifferent between A0 and A1. But since they are rational, they might play A0, or might play A1.

But in that case, if P1 is indifferent, and you play A0 with 60%, then P1 could play A0, or A1.

If P1 plays A0, then you get 5 if you play A0, 3 if you play A1 → so you'll play A0 → outcome is (A0, A0) → you get 5.

But if P1 plays A1, then you get 3 either way.

But to be **safe**, we assume that rational players will play best responses. And if P1 is indifferent, they might randomize, or play A0.

But to maximize your **expected payoff**, what should you choose?

Let’s compute expected payoff as a function of q.

Let’s suppose you play A0 with probability q.

Then P1’s best response:

- If 5q > 3 → plays A0
- If 5q < 3 → plays A1
- If 5q = 3 → indifferent → plays either

Now:

Case 1: q > 0.6 → P1 plays A0

Then your best response: play A0 (get 5), so you effectively play A0

Thus, payoff = 5

Case 2: q < 0.6 → P1 plays A1

Then your best response: play A1 (get 3)

Thus, payoff = 3

Case 3: q = 0.6 → 5q = 3 → P1 indifferent

Now, suppose P1 plays A0 with probability r, A1 with 1−r (r ∈ [0,1])

Then your expected payoff from playing A0:  
= r * 5 + (1−r) * 0 = 5r

Your expected payoff from playing A1:  
= r * 3 + (1−r) * 3 = 3

So you will prefer A0 if 5r > 3 → r > 0.6

A1 if r < 0.6

Indifferent if r = 0.6

But since P1 is indifferent, they might choose r = 1 (play A0), or r = 0 (play A1), or r = 0.6.

But to **maximize your payoff**, what is the **worst** possible r?

But we are not assuming worst-case — we assume rationality.

In game theory, when a player is indifferent, they can choose any. But to be safe, we assume they play a best response. Since they are indifferent, both are best responses.

But if you play q = 0.6, and P1 plays A1, you get 3.

If P1 plays A0, and you play A0, you get 5.

But you are choosing your strategy in advance.

You want to **maximize your expected payoff**.

So if you set q = 0.6, and assume that P1 *might* play A1 (even though indifferent), then your expected payoff could be less than 5.

But since P1 is rational and wants to maximize their own payoff, and if they play A1, they get 3. If they play A0, they get 5q = 5×0.6 = 3 → so same.

So they are indifferent.

Now, do they have a reason to play A0? Not necessarily — they might play A1.

But in fact, if P1 believes that you will play A0 with 60%, and plays A1, then their payoff is 3.

But they could play A0 and also get 3.

So no incentive.

But now, from your perspective: if you play q = 0.6, and P1 plays A1, then you get to respond with A1 (get 3).

If P1 plays A0, you get to respond with A0 (get 5).

But you don’t know what P1 does.

So expected payoff depends on what P1 does.

But if P1 is indifferent, they could play A0 with probability 1, or 0, or 0.5.

To be **safe**, or to maximize expected payoff, you cannot assume they play A0.

In fact, if P1 is rational, and you play q = 0.6, then P1 has 3 either way, so no reason to prefer one.

But if they play A1, then you get 3.

If they play A0, you get 5.

So your **expected payoff** depends on P1’s choice.

But since P1 is indifferent, we can assume they play any combination.

But this is **ambiguous**.

To **guarantee** a high payoff, it's safer to play **q = 1.0** (always play A0).

Then:
- P1 sees you always play A0 → so 5q = 5 > 3 → so P1’s best response is A0
- Then you play A0 → outcome (A0, A0) → you get 5

Perfect.

Similarly, if you play q = 0.6, and P1 plays A1 (which is a best response), then you get only 3.

So **to guarantee 5**, you should play A0 with 100% probability.

And since the other player is rational, they will play A0 in response.

Thus, you can **force** the outcome to (A0, A0), which gives you 5.

Is that true?

Yes — because:

- If you play A0 with probability 1 (q = 100%), then P1's expected payoff from A0 is 5×1 = 5, from A1 is 3 → so P1 strictly prefers A0 → so they play A0.

Then you, knowing that, will play A0 (since 5 > 3), so you end up with 5.

Therefore, **by playing A0 with 100% probability, you can obtain 5 points with certainty**.

If you play less than 100%, you risk the other player playing A1, which gives you only 3.

Therefore, the optimal strategy is to play A0 with 100% probability.

But wait: in the mixed strategy NE, both players mix — but in that case, you only get 3 expected payoff.

But that’s worse than 5.

So the mixed strategy NE is not best for you.

You can do better by playing A0 with certainty and inducing the other player to play A0.

And since this leads to a payoff of 5, which is higher than the 3 in mixed NE or A1,A1, this is superior.

Moreover, (A0,A0) is a Nash equilibrium, so if both players are rational, they can coordinate on it.

And since you are moving first in strategy choice (simultaneously), but you can choose your strategy to **induce** (A0,A0) as outcome.

Thus, **your optimal strategy is to play A0 with 100% probability.**

This gives you 5 points, guaranteed.

If you play A1 with any probability, you risk giving the other player incentive to play A1 (if you don’t play A0 often enough), and you get only 3.

So **100% A0 is best**.

### Final Answer:
